     <p>
      <b>
       Maize
      </b>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">
           ˈ
          </span>
          <span title="'m' in 'my'">
           m
          </span>
          <span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">
           eɪ
          </span>
          <span title="'z' in 'Zion'">
           z
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      <span title="English pronunciation respelling">
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i>
         <b>
          <span class="smallcaps">
           <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
            MAYZ
           </span>
          </span>
         </b>
        </i>
       </a>
      </span>
      ;
      <i>
       Zea mays
      </i>
      subsp.
      <i>
       mays
      </i>
      , from
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
       Spanish
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
       <i>
        maíz
       </i>
      </span>
      after
      <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_language" title="Taíno language">
       Taíno
      </a>
      <i>
       mahiz
      </i>
      ), also known as
      <b>
       corn
      </b>
      , is a large
      <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">
       grain
      </a>
      plant first domesticated by
      <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">
       indigenous peoples
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
       Mexico
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      about 10,000 years ago. The six major types of corn are
      <a href="/wiki/Dent_corn" title="Dent corn">
       dent corn
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Flint_corn" title="Flint corn">
       flint corn
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pod_corn" title="Pod corn">
       pod corn
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">
       popcorn
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Flour_corn" title="Flour corn">
       flour corn
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">
       sweet corn
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The leafy stalk of the plant produces separate pollen and
      <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">
       ovuliferous
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">
       inflorescences
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Ear_(botany)" title="Ear (botany)">
       ears
      </a>
      , which are fruits, yielding
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn_kernels" title="Corn kernels">
       kernels
      </a>
      (often erroneously called seeds). Maize kernels are often used in cooking as a
      <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">
       starch
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28">
        <a href="#Comparison_to_other_staple_foods">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          10
         </span>

          Comparison to other staple foods
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29">
        <a href="#Hazards">
         <span class="tocnumber">

         </span>

         </span>
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30">

         </li>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31">

        </ul>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32">

     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="800" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg/220px-Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg/330px-Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg/440px-Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Guil%C3%A1_Naquitz_Cave" title="Guilá Naquitz Cave">
         Guilá Naquitz Cave
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
         Oaxaca
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
         Mexico
        </a>
        is the site of early
        <a href="/wiki/Domestication#plants" title="Domestication">
         domestication
        </a>
        of several food crops, including
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">
         teosinte
        </a>
        (an ancestor of maize).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benz_3-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-benz-3">
          [3]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Most historians believe maize was domesticated in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tehuac%C3%A1n_Valley_matorral" title="Tehuacán Valley matorral">
       Tehuacan Valley
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
       Mexico
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recent research modified this view somewhat; scholars now indicate the adjacent
      <a href="/wiki/Balsas_River" title="Balsas River">
       Balsas River
      </a>
      Valley of south-central Mexico as the center of domestication.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">
       Olmec
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">
       Mayans
      </a>
      cultivated maize in numerous varieties throughout Mesoamerica, cooked, ground or processed through
      <a href="/wiki/Nixtamalization" title="Nixtamalization">
       nixtamalization
      </a>
      . Beginning about 2500 BC, the crop spread through much of the
      <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">
       Americas
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archsouth_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-archsouth-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The region developed a trade network based on surplus and varieties of maize crops.
     </p>
     <p>
      Nevertheless, recent data indicates that the spread of maize took place even earlier. According to Piperno,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       "A large corpus of data indicates that it [maize] was dispersed into lower Central America by 7600 BP [5600 BC] and had moved into the inter-Andean valleys of Colombia between 7000 and 6000 BP [5000-4000 BC]."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Since then, even earlier dates have been published.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pag.C3.A1n-Jim.C3.A9nezGuacham.C3.ADn-Tello2015_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pag.C3.A1n-Jim.C3.A9nezGuacham.C3.ADn-Tello2015-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After European contact with the Americas in the late 15th and early 16th centuries, explorers and traders
      <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">
       carried maize back to Europe
      </a>
      and introduced it to other countries. Maize spread to the rest of the world because of its ability to grow in diverse climates. Sugar-rich varieties called
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">
       sweet corn
      </a>
      are usually grown for human consumption as kernels, while
      <a href="/wiki/Field_corn" title="Field corn">
       field corn
      </a>
      varieties are used for animal feed, various corn-based human food uses (including grinding into
      <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">
       cornmeal
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Masa" title="Masa">
       masa
      </a>
      , pressing into
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_oil" title="Corn oil">
       corn oil
      </a>
      , and fermentation and distillation into alcoholic beverages like
      <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_whiskey" title="Bourbon whiskey">
       bourbon whiskey
      </a>
      ), and as chemical feedstocks.
     </p>
     <p>
      An influential 2002 study by Matsuoka
      <i>
       et al
      </i>
      . has demonstrated that, rather than the multiple independent domestications model, all maize arose from a single domestication in southern Mexico about 9,000 years ago. The study also demonstrated that the oldest surviving maize types are those of the Mexican highlands. Later, maize spread from this region over the Americas along two major paths. This is consistent with a model based on the archaeological record suggesting that maize diversified in the highlands of Mexico before spreading to the lowlands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mat_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mat-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencenow_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sciencenow-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Before they were domesticated, maize plants only grew small, 25 millimetres (1 in) long corn cobs, and only one per plant. Many centuries of artificial selection by the indigenous people of the Americas resulted in the development of maize plants capable of growing several cobs per plant that were usually several centimetres/inches long each.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is the most widely grown grain
      <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">
       crop
      </a>
      throughout the Americas,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOstats_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FAOstats-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with 332 million
      <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
       metric tons
      </a>
      grown annually in the United States alone. Approximately 40% of the crop—130 million tons—is used for corn
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">
       ethanol
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_maize" title="Genetically modified maize">
       Genetically modified maize
      </a>
      made up 85% of the maize planted in the United States in 2009.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GMOCompass_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GMOCompass-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
       Names
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corntassel_7095.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="800" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Corntassel_7095.jpg/170px-Corntassel_7095.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Corntassel_7095.jpg/255px-Corntassel_7095.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Corntassel_7095.jpg/340px-Corntassel_7095.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corntassel_7095.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Many small male flowers make up the male inflorescence, called the tassel.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The word
      <i>
       maize
      </i>
      derives from the Spanish form of the indigenous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_people" title="Taíno people">
       Taíno
      </a>
      word for the plant,
      <i>
       mahiz
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is known by other names around the world.
     </p>
     <p>
      The word "corn" outside North America, Australia, and New Zealand refers to any
      <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">
       cereal
      </a>
      crop, its meaning understood to vary geographically to refer to the local
      <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">
       staple
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_corn_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OED_corn-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the United States,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_corn_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-OED_corn-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Canada,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Australia, and New Zealand,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      <i>
       corn
      </i>
      primarily means maize; this usage started as a shortening of "Indian corn".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_corn_15-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-OED_corn-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Indian corn" primarily means maize (the staple grain of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Americans" title="Indigenous Americans">
       indigenous Americans
      </a>
      ), but can refer more specifically to multicolored "
      <a href="/wiki/Flint_corn" title="Flint corn">
       flint corn
      </a>
      " used for decoration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In places outside North America, Australia, and New Zealand,
      <i>
       corn
      </i>
      often refers to maize in culinary contexts. The narrower meaning is usually indicated by some additional word, as in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">
        sweet corn
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       sweetcorn
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Corn_on_the_cob" title="Corn on the cob">
        corn on the cob
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Baby_corn" title="Baby corn">
        baby corn
       </a>
      </i>
      , the puffed confection known as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">
        popcorn
       </a>
      </i>
      and the breakfast cereal known as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Corn_flakes" title="Corn flakes">
        corn flakes
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In Southern Africa, maize is commonly called
      <i>
       mielie
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">
       Afrikaans
      </a>
      ) or
      <i>
       mealie
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
       English
      </a>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_mealie_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OED_mealie-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      words derived from the Portuguese word for maize,
      <i>
       milho
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Maize
      </i>
      is preferred in formal, scientific, and international usage because it refers specifically to this one grain, unlike
      <i>
       corn
      </i>
      , which has a complex variety of meanings that vary by context and geographic region.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Maize
      </i>
      is used by agricultural bodies and
      <a href="/wiki/Research_institute" title="Research institute">
       research institutes
      </a>
      such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO">
       FAO
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CSIRO" title="CSIRO">
       CSIRO
      </a>
      . National agricultural and industry associations often include the word
      <i>
       maize
      </i>
      in their name even in English-speaking countries where the local, informal word is something other than
      <i>
       maize
      </i>
      ; for example, the Maize Association of Australia, the Indian Maize Development Association, the Kenya Maize Consortium and Maize Breeders Network, the National Maize Association of Nigeria, the Zimbabwe Seed Maize Association. However, in commodities trading,
      <i>
       corn
      </i>
      consistently refers to maize and not other grains.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_physiology">
       Structure and physiology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The maize plant is often 3 m (10 ft) in height,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      though some natural strains can grow 12 m (39 ft).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The stem is commonly composed of 20
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internode_(botany)" title="Internode (botany)">
       internodes
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of 18 cm (7.1 in) length.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A leaf, which grows from each node, is generally 9 cm (4 in) in width and 120 cm (4 ft) in length.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ears develop above a few of the leaves in the midsection of the plant, between the stem and leaf sheath, elongating by ~3 mm/day, to a length of 18 cm (7 in)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with 60 cm (24 in) being the maximum alleged in the subspecies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are female
      <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">
       inflorescences
      </a>
      , tightly enveloped by several layers of ear leaves commonly called husks. Certain varieties of maize have been bred to produce many additional developed ears. These are the source of the "
      <a href="/wiki/Baby_corn" title="Baby corn">
       baby corn
      </a>
      " used as a vegetable in
      <a href="/wiki/Asian_cuisine" title="Asian cuisine">
       Asian cuisine
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The apex of the stem ends in the tassel, an
      <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">
       inflorescence
      </a>
      of male flowers. When the tassel is mature and conditions are suitably warm and dry, anthers on the tassel
      <a href="/wiki/Dehiscence_(botany)" title="Dehiscence (botany)">
       dehisce
      </a>
      and release pollen. Maize pollen is
      <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">
       anemophilous
      </a>
      (dispersed by wind), and because of its large settling velocity, most pollen falls within a few meters of the tassel.
     </p>
     <p>
      Elongated
      <a href="/wiki/Stigma_(botany)" title="Stigma (botany)">
       stigmas
      </a>
      , called
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_silk" title="Corn silk">
       silks
      </a>
      , emerge from the whorl of husk leaves at the end of the ear. They are often pale yellow and 18 cm (7 in) in length, like tufts of hair in appearance. At the end of each is a carpel, which may develop into a "kernel" if fertilized by a pollen grain. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericarp" title="Pericarp">
       pericarp
      </a>
      of the fruit is fused with the seed coat referred to as "
      <a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">
       caryopsis
      </a>
      ", typical of the
      <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">
       grasses
      </a>
      , and the entire kernel is often referred to as the "
      <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">
       seed
      </a>
      ". The cob is close to a
      <a href="/wiki/Multiple_fruit" title="Multiple fruit">
       multiple fruit
      </a>
      in structure, except that the individual fruits (the kernels) never fuse into a single mass. The grains are about the size of
      <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">
       peas
      </a>
      , and adhere in regular rows around a white, pithy substance, which forms the ear. The maximum size of kernels is reputedly 2.5 cm (1 in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An ear commonly holds 600 kernels. They are of various colors: blackish,
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_corn" title="Blue corn">
       bluish-gray
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Purple_corn" title="Purple corn">
       purple
      </a>
      , green, red, white and yellow. When ground into
      <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">
       flour
      </a>
      , maize yields more flour with much less
      <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">
       bran
      </a>
      than wheat does. It lacks the protein
      <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">
       gluten
      </a>
      of wheat and, therefore, makes baked goods with poor rising capability. A
      <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">
       genetic variant
      </a>
      that accumulates more sugar and less
      <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">
       starch
      </a>
      in the ear is consumed as a vegetable and is called
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">
       sweet corn
      </a>
      . Young ears can be consumed raw, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Corncob" title="Corncob">
       cob
      </a>
      and silk, but as the plant matures (usually during the summer months), the cob becomes tougher and the silk dries to inedibility. By the end of the
      <a href="/wiki/Growing_season" title="Growing season">
       growing season
      </a>
      , the kernels dry out and become difficult to chew without cooking them tender first in boiling water.
     </p>
     <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 89.333333333333px">
       <div style="width: 89.333333333333px">
        <div class="thumb" style="width: 87.333333333333px;">
         <div style="margin:0px auto;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cornsilk_7091.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1097" data-file-width="800" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Cornsilk_7091.jpg/131px-Cornsilk_7091.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Cornsilk_7091.jpg/197px-Cornsilk_7091.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Cornsilk_7091.jpg/262px-Cornsilk_7091.jpg 2x" width="88"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="gallerytext">
         <p>
          Female inflorescence, with young
          <a href="/wiki/Corn_silk" title="Corn silk">
           silk
          </a>
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
      </li>
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182.66666666667px">

         </p>

         </p>

      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 83.333333333333px">
       <div style="width: 83.333333333333px">
        <div class="thumb" style="width: 81.333333333333px;">
         <div style="margin:0px auto;">

         </p>

      </li>
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 92px">
       <div style="width: 92px">
        <div class="thumb" style="width: 90px;">
         <div style="margin:0px auto;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_(Croatia).JPG">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG/135px-Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG/202px-Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG/270px-Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG 2x" width="90"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="gallerytext">
         <p>
          Mature maize ear on a stalk
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Planting density affects multiple aspects of maize. Modern farming techniques in
      <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">
       developed countries
      </a>
      usually rely on dense planting, which produces one ear per stalk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stands of
      <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">
       silage
      </a>
      maize are yet denser,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and achieve a lower percentage of ears and more plant matter.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-night_plant" title="Long-night plant">
       facultative short-day plant
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and flowers in a certain number of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Growing_degree_day" title="Growing degree day">
       growing degree days
      </a>
      &gt; 10 °C (50 °F) in the environment to which it is adapted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The magnitude of the influence that long nights have on the number of days that must pass before maize
      <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">
       flowers
      </a>
      is genetically prescribed
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and regulated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Phytochrome" title="Phytochrome">
       phytochrome
      </a>
      system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">
       Photoperiodicity
      </a>
      can be eccentric in tropical
      <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">
       cultivars
      </a>
      such that the long days characteristic of higher latitudes allow the plants to grow so tall that they do not have enough time to produce seed before being killed by frost. These attributes, however, may prove useful in using tropical maize for
      <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">
       biofuels
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Immature maize shoots accumulate a powerful antibiotic substance, 2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one (
      <a href="/wiki/DIMBOA" title="DIMBOA">
       DIMBOA
      </a>
      ). DIMBOA is a member of a group of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydroxamic_acid" title="Hydroxamic acid">
       hydroxamic acids
      </a>
      (also known as benzoxazinoids) that serve as a natural defense against a wide range of pests, including insects,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogenic" title="Pathogenic">
       pathogenic
      </a>
      fungi and
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      . DIMBOA is also found in related grasses, particularly wheat. A maize mutant (bx) lacking DIMBOA is highly susceptible to attack by
      <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">
       aphids
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">
       fungi
      </a>
      . DIMBOA is also responsible for the relative resistance of immature maize to the
      <a href="/wiki/European_corn_borer" title="European corn borer">
       European corn borer
      </a>
      (family
      <a href="/wiki/Crambidae" title="Crambidae">
       Crambidae
      </a>
      ). As maize matures, DIMBOA levels and resistance to the corn borer decline.
     </p>
     <p>
      Because of its shallow roots, maize is susceptible to droughts, intolerant of nutrient-deficient soils, and prone to be uprooted by severe winds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <div class="thumb tright">

       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_(Zea_mays)_MHNT_2.jpg">

       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">

        </i>

        </a>
