     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corncobs.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Corncobs.jpg/220px-Corncobs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Corncobs.jpg/330px-Corncobs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Corncobs.jpg/440px-Corncobs.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corncobs.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Variegated maize ears
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg/220px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg/330px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg/440px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Multicolored corn kernels (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CSIRO" title="CSIRO">
         CSIRO
        </a>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      While yellow maizes derive their color from
      <a href="/wiki/Lutein" title="Lutein">
       lutein
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Zeaxanthin" title="Zeaxanthin">
       zeaxanthin
      </a>
      , in red-colored maizes, the kernel coloration is due to
      <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">
       anthocyanins
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Phlobaphene" title="Phlobaphene">
       phlobaphenes
      </a>
      . These latter substances are synthesized in the flavonoids synthetic pathway
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      from polymerisation of
      <a href="/wiki/Flavan-4-ol" title="Flavan-4-ol">
       flavan-4-ols
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      by the expression of maize pericarp color1 (p1) gene
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chopra2003_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chopra2003-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which encodes an R2R3
      <a href="/wiki/MYB_(gene)" title="MYB (gene)">
       myb
      </a>
      -like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcriptional_activator" title="Transcriptional activator">
       transcriptional activator
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of the A1 gene encoding for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihydroflavonol_4-reductase" title="Dihydroflavonol 4-reductase">
       dihydroflavonol 4-reductase
      </a>
      (reducing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihydroflavonol" title="Dihydroflavonol">
       dihydroflavonols
      </a>
      into flavan-4-ols)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while another gene (Suppressor of Pericarp Pigmentation 1 or SPP1) acts as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcriptional_suppressor" title="Transcriptional suppressor">
       suppressor
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The p1 gene encodes an Myb-homologous transcriptional activator of genes required for biosynthesis of red phlobaphene pigments, while the P1-wr allele specifies colorless kernel pericarp and red cobs, and unstable factor for orange1 (Ufo1) modifies P1-wr expression to confer pigmentation in kernel pericarp, as well as vegetative tissues, which normally do not accumulate significant amounts of phlobaphene pigments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chopra2003_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chopra2003-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The maize P gene encodes a Myb homolog that recognizes the sequence CCT/AACC, in sharp contrast with the C/TAACGG bound by vertebrate Myb proteins.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Abnormal_flowers">
       Abnormal flowers
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Sometimes in maize,
      <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">
       inflorescences
      </a>
      are found containing both male and female flowers, or
      <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">
       hermaphrodite
      </a>
      flowers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">
       Genetics
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GEM_corn.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1864" data-file-width="2790" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/GEM_corn.jpg/220px-GEM_corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/GEM_corn.jpg/330px-GEM_corn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/GEM_corn.jpg/440px-GEM_corn.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GEM_corn.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Exotic varieties of maize are collected to add
        <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">
         genetic diversity
        </a>
        when selectively breeding new domestic strains
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many forms of maize are used for food, sometimes classified as various subspecies related to the amount of starch each has:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Flour corn:
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        amylacea
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">
        Popcorn
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        everta
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Dent_corn" title="Dent corn">
        Dent corn
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        indentata
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Flint_corn" title="Flint corn">
        Flint corn
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        indurata
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">
        Sweet corn
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        saccharata
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        rugosa
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Waxy_corn" title="Waxy corn">
        Waxy corn
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        ceratina
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Amylomaize" title="Amylomaize">
        Amylomaize
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Pod_corn" title="Pod corn">
        Pod corn
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        tunicata
       </i>
       Larrañaga ex A. St. Hil.
      </li>
      <li>
       Striped maize:
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       var.
       <i>
        japonica
       </i>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      This system has been replaced (though not entirely displaced) over the last 60 years by multivariable classifications based on ever more data.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomics" title="Agronomics">
       Agronomic
      </a>
      data were supplemented by botanical traits for a robust initial classification, then genetic,
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">
       cytological
      </a>
      , protein and DNA evidence was added. Now, the categories are forms (little used), races, racial complexes, and recently branches.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">
       diploid
      </a>
      with 20
      <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">
       chromosomes
      </a>
      (n=10). The combined length of the chromosomes is 1500
      <a href="/wiki/Centimorgan" title="Centimorgan">
       cM
      </a>
      . Some of the maize chromosomes have what are known as "chromosomal knobs": highly repetitive
      <a href="/wiki/Heterochromatin" title="Heterochromatin">
       heterochromatic
      </a>
      domains that stain darkly. Individual knobs are
      <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">
       polymorphic
      </a>
      among strains of both maize and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">
       teosinte
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Barbara_McClintock" title="Barbara McClintock">
       Barbara McClintock
      </a>
      used these knob markers to validate her
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transposon" title="Transposon">
       transposon
      </a>
      theory of "jumping genes", for which she won the 1983
      <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">
       Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine
      </a>
      . Maize is still an important
      <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">
       model organism
      </a>
      for genetics and
      <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">
       developmental biology
      </a>
      today.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Maize Genetics Cooperation Stock Center, funded by the USDA
      <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">
       Agricultural Research Service
      </a>
      and located in the Department of Crop Sciences at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign">
       University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign
      </a>
      , is a stock center of maize mutants. The total collection has nearly 80,000 samples. The bulk of the collection consists of several hundred named genes, plus additional gene combinations and other heritable variants. There are about 1000 chromosomal aberrations (e.g., translocations and inversions) and stocks with abnormal chromosome numbers (e.g.,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraploid" title="Tetraploid">
       tetraploids
      </a>
      ). Genetic data describing the maize mutant stocks as well as myriad other data about maize genetics can be accessed at
      <a class="external text" href="http://maizegdb.org/" rel="nofollow">
       MaizeGDB
      </a>
      , the Maize Genetics and Genomics Database.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2005, the US
      <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">
       National Science Foundation
      </a>
      (NSF), Department of Agriculture (
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">
       USDA
      </a>
      ) and the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
       Department of Energy
      </a>
      (DOE) formed a consortium to sequence the B73 maize
      <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">
       genome
      </a>
      . The resulting DNA sequence data was deposited immediately into
      <a href="/wiki/GenBank" title="GenBank">
       GenBank
      </a>
      , a public repository for genome-sequence data. Sequences and genome annotations have also been made available throughout the project's lifetime at the project's official site.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Primary sequencing of the maize genome was completed in 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On November 20, 2009, the consortium published results of its sequencing effort in
      <i>
       Science
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The genome, 85% of which is composed of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transposon" title="Transposon">
       transposons
      </a>
      , was found to contain 32,540 genes (By comparison, the
      <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">
       human genome
      </a>
      contains about 2.9 billion bases and 26,000 genes). Much of the maize genome has been duplicated and reshuffled by
      <a href="/wiki/Helitron_(biology)" title="Helitron (biology)">
       helitrons
      </a>
      —group of rolling circle transposons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to a genetic study by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embrapa" title="Embrapa">
       Embrapa
      </a>
      , corn cultivation was introduced in South America from Mexico, in two great waves: the first, 5000 years ago, spread through the
      <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">
       Andes
      </a>
      ; the second, about 2000 years ago, through the lowlands of South America.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">
       Breeding
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Field,_corn,_Liechtenstein,_Mountains,_Alps,_Vaduz,_sky,_clouds,_landscape.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg/220px-Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg/330px-Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg/440px-Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field,_corn,_Liechtenstein,_Mountains,_Alps,_Vaduz,_sky,_clouds,_landscape.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Field of maize in
        <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">
         Liechtenstein
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize reproduces sexually each year. This randomly selects half the genes from a given plant to propagate to the next generation, meaning that desirable traits found in the crop (like high yield or good nutrition) can be lost in subsequent generations unless certain techniques are used.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize breeding in prehistory resulted in large plants producing large ears. Modern breeding began with individuals who selected highly productive varieties in their fields and then sold seed to other farmers. James L. Reid was one of the earliest and most successful developing Reid's Yellow Dent in the 1860s. These early efforts were based on
      <a href="/wiki/Selection_methods_in_plant_breeding_based_on_mode_of_reproduction" title="Selection methods in plant breeding based on mode of reproduction">
       mass selection
      </a>
      . Later breeding efforts included ear to row selection, (C. G. Hopkins ca. 1896), hybrids made from selected
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inbred" title="Inbred">
       inbred
      </a>
      lines (G. H. Shull, 1909), and the highly successful double cross hybrids using 4 inbred lines (
      <a href="/wiki/Donald_F._Jones" title="Donald F. Jones">
       D. F. Jones
      </a>
      ca. 1918, 1922). University supported breeding programs were especially important in developing and introducing modern hybrids. (Ref Jugenheimer Hybrid Maize Breeding and Seed Production pub. 1958) by the 1930s, companies such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pioneer_Hi-Bred" title="Pioneer Hi-Bred">
       Pioneer
      </a>
      devoted to production of hybrid maize had begun to influence long term development. Internationally important seed banks such as
      <a href="/wiki/International_Maize_and_Wheat_Improvement_Center" title="International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center">
       International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center
      </a>
      (CIMMYT) and the US bank at Maize Genetics Cooperation Stock Center
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign">
       University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign
      </a>
      maintain
      <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">
       germplasm
      </a>
      important for future crop development.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the 1940s the best strains of maize have been first-generation hybrids made from inbred strains that have been optimized for specific traits, such as yield, nutrition, drought, pest and disease tolerance. Both conventional cross-breeding and genetic modification have succeeded in increasing output and reducing the need for cropland, pesticides, water and fertilizer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt14_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt14-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Global_maize_program">
       Global maize program
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2323" data-file-width="10072" height="51" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg/220px-2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg/330px-2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg/440px-2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Panorama of cornfields in
        <a href="/wiki/Nan_Province" title="Nan Province">
         Nan Province
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
         Thailand
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      CIMMYT operates a conventional breeding program to provide optimized strains. The program began in the 1980s. Hybrid seeds are distributed in Africa by the Drought Tolerant Maize for Africa project.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt14_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt14-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_modification">
       Genetic modification
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_maize" title="Transgenic maize">
       Transgenic maize
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">
       Genetically modified
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_maize" title="Genetically modified maize">
       (GM) maize
      </a>
      is one of the 25
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">
       GM crops
      </a>
      grown commercially in 2011.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Grown since 1997 in the United States and Canada, 86% of the US maize crop was genetically modified in 2010
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and 32% of the worldwide maize crop was GM in 2011.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of 2011, Herbicide-tolerant maize varieties are grown in Argentina, Australia, Brazil, Canada, China, Colombia, El Salvador, the EU, Honduras, Japan, Korea, Malaysia, Mexico, New Zealand, Philippines, the Russian Federation, Singapore, South Africa, Taiwan, Thailand, and USA, and insect-resistant corn is grown in Argentina, Australia, Brazil, Canada, Chile, China, Colombia, Czech Republic, Egypt, the EU, Honduras, Japan, Korea, Malaysia, Mexico, Netherlands, New Zealand, Philippines, Romania, Russian Federation, South Africa, Switzerland, Taiwan, USA, and Uruguay.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In September 2000, up to $50 million worth of food products were recalled due to contamination with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starlink_corn_recall" title="Starlink corn recall">
       Starlink
      </a>
      genetically modified corn, which had been approved only for animal consumption and had not been approved for human consumption, and was subsequently withdrawn from the market.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">
       Origin
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte#Origin_of_maize_and_interaction_with_teosintes" title="Teosinte">
       Origin of maize and interaction with teosintes
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corn_parents1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="1800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Corn_parents1.jpg/220px-Corn_parents1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Corn_parents1.jpg/330px-Corn_parents1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Corn_parents1.jpg/440px-Corn_parents1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corn_parents1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A
        <i>
         Tripsacum
        </i>
        grass (big) and a teosinte (small)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize is the domesticated variant of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">
       teosinte
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corn_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-corn-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The two plants have dissimilar appearance, maize having a single tall stalk with multiple leaves and teosinte being a short, bushy plant. The difference between the two is largely controlled by differences in just two genes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corn_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-corn-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Several theories had been proposed about the specific origin of maize in Mesoamerica:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkes_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wilkes-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ol>
      <li>
       It is a direct
       <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">
        domestication
       </a>
       of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_people" title="Mexican people">
        Mexican
       </a>
       annual
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">
        teosinte
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       ssp.
       <i>
        parviglumis
       </i>
       , native to the
       <a href="/wiki/Balsas_River" title="Balsas River">
        Balsas River
       </a>
       valley in south-eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       , with up to 12% of its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_material" title="Genetic material">
        genetic material
       </a>
       obtained from
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       ssp.
       <i>
        mexicana
       </i>
       through
       <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">
        introgression
       </a>
       . This theory was further confirmed by the 2002 study of Matsuoka et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mat_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mat-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       It has been derived from hybridization between a small domesticated maize (a slightly changed form of a wild maize) and a teosinte of section
       <i>
        Luxuriantes
       </i>
       , either
       <i>
        Z. luxurians
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Zea_diploperennis" title="Zea diploperennis">
         Z. diploperennis
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       It has undergone two or more domestications either of a wild maize or of a teosinte. (The term "teosinte" describes all
       <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">
        species
       </a>
       and subspecies in the genus
       <i>
        Zea
       </i>
       , excluding
       <i>
        Zea mays
       </i>
       ssp.
       <i>
        mays
       </i>
       .)
      </li>
      <li>
       It has evolved from a hybridization of
       <i>
        Z. diploperennis
       </i>
       by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tripsacum_dactyloides" title="Tripsacum dactyloides">
         Tripsacum dactyloides
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </li>
     </ol>
     <p>
      In the late 1930s, Paul Mangelsdorf suggested that domesticated maize was the result of a hybridization event between an unknown wild maize and a species of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tripsacum" title="Tripsacum">
        Tripsacum
       </a>
      </i>
      , a related genus. This theory about the origin of maize has been refuted by modern
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">
       genetic testing
      </a>
      , which refutes Mangelsdorf's model and the fourth listed above.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkes_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-wilkes-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :40
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The teosinte origin theory was proposed by the Russian botanist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolai_Ivanovich_Vavilov" title="Nikolai Ivanovich Vavilov">
       Nikolai Ivanovich Vavilov
      </a>
      in 1931 and the later American
      <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">
       Nobel Prize
      </a>
      -winner
      <a href="/wiki/George_Beadle" title="George Beadle">
       George Beadle
      </a>
      in 1932.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkes_56-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-wilkes-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :10
      </sup>
      It is supported experimentally and by recent studies of the plants' genomes. Teosinte and maize are able to cross-breed and produce fertile offspring. A number of questions remain concerning the species, among them:
     </p>
     <ol>
      <li>
       how the immense diversity of the species of sect.
       <i>
        Zea
       </i>
       originated,
      </li>
      <li>
       how the tiny archaeological specimens of 3500–2700 BC could have been selected from a teosinte, and
      </li>
      <li>
       how domestication could have proceeded without leaving remains of teosinte or maize with teosintoid traits earlier than the earliest known until recently, dating from ca. 1100 BC.
      </li>
     </ol>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">
       domestication
      </a>
      of maize is of particular interest to researchers—
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeologist" title="Archaeologist">
       archaeologists
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">
       geneticists
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ethnobotany" title="Ethnobotany">
       ethnobotanists
      </a>
      , geographers, etc. The process is thought by some to have started 7,500 to 12,000 years ago. Research from the 1950s to 1970s originally focused on the hypothesis that maize domestication occurred in the highlands between the states of
      <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
       Oaxaca
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Jalisco" title="Jalisco">
       Jalisco
      </a>
      , because the oldest archaeological remains of maize known at the time were found there.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Connection_with_.27parviglumis.27_subspecies">
       Connection with 'parviglumis' subspecies
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1037" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/220px-Maize-teosinte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/330px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/440px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">
         teosinte
        </a>
        (top), maize-teosinte hybrid (middle), maize (bottom)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Genetic studies led by
      <a href="/wiki/John_Doebley" title="John Doebley">
       John Doebley
      </a>
      identified
      <i>
       Zea mays
      </i>
      ssp.
      <i>
       parviglumis
      </i>
      , native to the
      <a href="/wiki/Balsas_River" title="Balsas River">
       Balsas River
      </a>
      valley in Mexico's southwestern highlands, and also known as Balsas teosinte, as being the
      <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relative" title="Crop wild relative">
       crop wild relative
      </a>
      teosinte genetically most similar to modern maize.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has been confirmed by further more recent studies, which refined this hypothesis somewhat. Archaeobotanical studies published in 2009 now point to the middle part of the Balsas River valley as the more likely location of early domestication; this river is not very long, so these locations are not very distant. Stone milling tools with maize residue have been found in an 8,700-years old layer of deposits in a cave not far from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iguala,_Guerrero" title="Iguala, Guerrero">
       Iguala, Guerrero
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranere_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ranere-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Also, Doebley was part of the team that is credited with first finding, back in 2002, that maize had been domesticated only once, about 9000 years ago, and then spread throughout the Americas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mat_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mat-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A primitive corn was being grown in southern Mexico, Central America, and northern South America 7,000 years ago. Archaeological remains of early maize ears, found at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guila_Naquitz_Cave" title="Guila Naquitz Cave">
       Guila Naquitz Cave
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca_Valley" title="Oaxaca Valley">
       Oaxaca Valley
      </a>
      , date back roughly 6,250 years; the oldest ears from caves near
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tehuac%C3%A1n,_Puebla" title="Tehuacán, Puebla">
       Tehuacan
      </a>
      , Puebla, date ca. 3,450 BC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archsouth_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-archsouth-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize pollen dated to 7300 cal B.P. from
      <a href="/wiki/San_Andr%C3%A9s_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="San Andrés (Mesoamerican site)">
       San Andres, Tabasco
      </a>
      , on the Caribbean coast has also been recovered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranere_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ranere-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As maize was introduced to new cultures, new uses were developed and new varieties selected to better serve in those preparations. Maize was the staple food, or a major staple – along with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">
       squash
      </a>
      , Andean region
      <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">
       potato
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Quinoa" title="Quinoa">
       quinoa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">
       beans
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Amaranth" title="Amaranth">
       amaranth
      </a>
      – of most
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">
       pre-Columbian
      </a>
      North American, Mesoamerican, South American, and Caribbean cultures. The Mesoamerican civilization, in particular, was deeply interrelated with maize. Its traditions and rituals involved all aspects of maize cultivation – from the planting to the food preparation. Maize formed the Mesoamerican people's identity.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Centeotl.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="410" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Centeotl.jpg/220px-Centeotl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Centeotl.jpg/330px-Centeotl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Centeotl.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Centeotl.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Centeotl" title="Centeotl">
         Centeotl
        </a>
        , the Aztec deity of maize
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      It is unknown what precipitated its domestication, because the edible portion of the wild variety is too small and hard to obtain to be eaten directly, as each kernel is enclosed in a very hard bivalve shell. It is possible that, early on, teosinte may have been gathered as preferred feed for domestic animals.
     </p>
     <p>
      Also, back in 1939, George Beadle demonstrated that the kernels of teosinte are readily "popped" for human consumption, like modern popcorn.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some have argued it would have taken too many generations of
      <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">
       selective breeding
      </a>
      to produce large, compressed ears for efficient cultivation. However, studies of the hybrids readily made by intercrossing teosinte and modern maize suggest this objection is not well founded.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spreading_to_the_north">
       Spreading to the north
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Around 2500 BC, maize began to spread to the north; it was first cultivated in what is now the United States at several sites in New Mexico and Arizona, about 2100 BC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the first millennium AD, maize cultivation spread more widely in the areas north. In particular, the large-scale adoption of maize agriculture and consumption in eastern North America took place about A.D. 900. Native Americans cleared large forest and grassland areas for the new crop.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2005, research by the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">
       USDA
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">
       Forest Service
      </a>
      suggested that the rise in maize cultivation 500 to 1,000 years ago in what is now the southeastern United States corresponded with a decline of freshwater
      <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">
       mussels
      </a>
      , which are very sensitive to environmental changes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Production">
       Production
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">
       Methods
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="851" data-file-width="952" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG/220px-Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG/330px-Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG/440px-Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Seedlings three weeks after sowing
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg/170px-Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg/255px-Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg/340px-Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Young stalks
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Because it is cold-intolerant, in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">
       temperate zones
      </a>
      maize must be planted in the spring. Its
      <a href="/wiki/Root_system" title="Root system">
       root system
      </a>
      is generally shallow, so the plant is dependent on soil moisture. As a C4 plant (a plant that uses
      <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">
       C4 carbon fixation
      </a>
      ), maize is a considerably more water-efficient crop than C3 plants (plants that use
      <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">
       C3 carbon fixation
      </a>
      ) like the small grains,
      <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">
       alfalfa
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybeans" title="Soybeans">
       soybeans
      </a>
      . Maize is most sensitive to drought at the time of silk emergence, when the flowers are ready for pollination. In the United States, a good harvest was traditionally predicted if the maize were "knee-high by the
      <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">
       Fourth of July
      </a>
      ", although modern
      <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">
       hybrids
      </a>
      generally exceed this growth rate. Maize used for
      <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">
       silage
      </a>
      is harvested while the plant is green and the fruit immature. Sweet corn is harvested in the "milk stage", after pollination but before starch has formed, between late summer and early to mid-autumn. Field maize is left in the field very late in the autumn to thoroughly dry the grain, and may, in fact, sometimes not be harvested until winter or even early spring. The importance of sufficient soil moisture is shown in many parts of
      <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
       Africa
      </a>
      , where periodic
      <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">
       drought
      </a>
      regularly causes maize crop failure and consequent
      <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">
       famine
      </a>
      . Although it is grown mainly in wet, hot climates, it has been said to thrive in cold, hot, dry or wet conditions, meaning that it is an extremely versatile crop.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maispflanze.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Maispflanze.jpg/170px-Maispflanze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Maispflanze.jpg/255px-Maispflanze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Maispflanze.jpg/340px-Maispflanze.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maispflanze.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Mature plants showing ears
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize was planted by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_(Americas)" title="Native American (Americas)">
       Native Americans
      </a>
      in hills, in a complex system known to some as the
      <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">
       Three Sisters
      </a>
      . Maize provided support for
      <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">
       beans
      </a>
      , and the beans provided nitrogen derived from nitrogen-fixing
      <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">
       rhizobia
      </a>
      bacteria which live on the roots of beans and other
      <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">
       legumes
      </a>
      ; and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(fruit)" title="Squash (fruit)">
       squashes
      </a>
      provided ground cover to stop weeds and inhibit evaporation by providing shade over the soil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann_2011_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mann_2011-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This method was replaced by single species hill planting where each hill 60–120 cm (2.0–3.9 ft) apart was planted with three or four seeds, a method still used by home gardeners. A later technique was "checked maize", where hills were placed 40 in (1.0 m) apart in each direction, allowing cultivators to run through the field in two directions. In more arid lands, this was altered and seeds were planted in the bottom of 10–12 cm (3.9–4.7 in) deep furrows to collect water. Modern technique plants maize in rows which allows for cultivation while the plant is young, although the hill technique is still used in the maize fields of some Native American reservations. When maize is planted in rows, it also allows for planting of other crops between these rows to make more efficient use of land space.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In North America, fields are often planted in a two-
      <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">
       crop rotation
      </a>
      with a
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">
       nitrogen-fixing
      </a>
      crop, often
      <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">
       alfalfa
      </a>
      in cooler climates and
      <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">
       soybeans
      </a>
      in regions with longer summers. Sometimes a third crop,
      <a href="/wiki/Winter_wheat" title="Winter wheat">
       winter wheat
      </a>
      , is added to the rotation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many of the maize varieties grown in the United States and Canada are hybrids. Often the varieties have been
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">
       genetically modified
      </a>
      to tolerate
      <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">
       glyphosate
      </a>
      or to provide protection against natural pests. Glyphosate is an herbicide which kills all plants except those with genetic tolerance. This genetic tolerance is very rarely found in nature.
     </p>
     <p>
      In midwestern United States, low-till or
      <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">
       no-till farming
      </a>
      techniques are usually used. In low-till, fields are covered once, maybe twice, with a tillage implement either ahead of crop planting or after the previous harvest. The fields are planted and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anhydrous_ammonia" title="Anhydrous ammonia">
       fertilized
      </a>
      . Weeds are controlled through the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">
       herbicides
      </a>
      , and no cultivation tillage is done during the growing season. This technique reduces moisture evaporation from the soil, and thus provides more moisture for the crop. The technologies mentioned in the previous paragraph enable low-till and no-till farming. Weeds compete with the crop for moisture and nutrients, making them undesirable.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YellowCorn.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2167" data-file-width="3032" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/YellowCorn.jpg/220px-YellowCorn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/YellowCorn.jpg/330px-YellowCorn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/YellowCorn.jpg/440px-YellowCorn.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YellowCorn.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Mature field maize ears
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Before
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      , most maize in North America was harvested by hand. This involves a large numbers of workers and associated social events (husking or shucking
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bee_(gathering)" title="Bee (gathering)">
       bees
      </a>
      ). Some one- and two-row mechanical pickers were in use, but the maize
      <a href="/wiki/Combine_harvester" title="Combine harvester">
       combine
      </a>
      was not adopted until after the War. By hand or mechanical picker, the entire ear is harvested, which then requires a separate operation of a maize sheller to remove the kernels from the ear. Whole ears of maize were often stored in
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_crib" title="Corn crib">
       corn cribs
      </a>
      , and these whole ears are a sufficient form for some livestock feeding use. Few modern farms store maize in this manner. Most harvest the grain from the field and store it in bins. The combine with a corn head (with points and snap rolls instead of a reel) does not cut the stalk; it simply pulls the stalk down. The stalk continues downward and is crumpled into a mangled pile on the ground. The ear of maize is too large to pass between slots in a plate as the snap rolls pull the stalk away, leaving only the ear and husk to enter the machinery. The combine separates out the husk and the cob, keeping only the kernels.
     </p>
     <p>
      For storing grain in bins, the moisture of the grain must be sufficiently low to avoid spoiling. If the moisture content of the harvested grain is too high,
      <a href="/wiki/Grain_drying" title="Grain drying">
       grain dryers
      </a>
      are used to reduce the moisture content by blowing heated air through the grain. This can require large amounts of energy in the form of combustible gases (
      <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">
       propane
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      ) and electricity to power the blowers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Quantity">
       Quantity
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaizeYield.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1600" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/MaizeYield.png/220px-MaizeYield.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/MaizeYield.png/330px-MaizeYield.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/MaizeYield.png/440px-MaizeYield.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaizeYield.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Worldwide maize production
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1110" data-file-width="1992" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg/220px-Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg/330px-Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg/440px-Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Harvesting maize during the record 2009 season in
        <a href="/wiki/Jones_County,_Iowa" title="Jones County, Iowa">
         Jones County, Iowa
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_(8641099254).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2988" data-file-width="4226" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_%288641099254%29.jpg/220px-CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_%288641099254%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_%288641099254%29.jpg/330px-CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_%288641099254%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_%288641099254%29.jpg/440px-CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_%288641099254%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CORN_ON_THE_COB_BEIJING_CHINA_OCT_2012_(8641099254).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Maize in Beijing, China, October 2012
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize is widely cultivated throughout the world, and a greater weight of maize is produced each year than any other grain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The United States produces 40% of the world's harvest; other top producing countries include China, Brazil,
      <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
       Mexico
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
       Indonesia
      </a>
      , India, France and
      <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
       Argentina
      </a>
      . Worldwide production was 817 million
      <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
       tonnes
      </a>
      in 2009—more than
      <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">
       rice
      </a>
      (678 million
      <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
       tonnes
      </a>
      ) or
      <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">
       wheat
      </a>
      (682 million
      <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
       tonnes
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOstats_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FAOstats-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2009, over 159 million
      <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">
       hectares
      </a>
      (390 million acres) of maize were planted worldwide, with a yield of over 5 tonnes per hectare (80 bu/acre). Production can be significantly higher in certain regions of the world; 2009 forecasts for production in Iowa were 11614 kg/ha (185 bu/acre).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [Note 1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is conflicting evidence to support the hypothesis that maize yield potential has increased over the past few decades. This suggests that changes in yield potential are associated with leaf angle, lodging resistance, tolerance of high plant density, disease/pest tolerance, and other agronomic traits rather than increase of yield potential per individual plant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
       United States
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corn_belt.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="844" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Corn_belt.svg/400px-Corn_belt.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Corn_belt.svg/600px-Corn_belt.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Corn_belt.svg/800px-Corn_belt.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corn_belt.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Corn production by county in the United States, 2010
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_production_in_the_United_States" title="Corn production in the United States">
       Corn production in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2010, the maize planted area for all purposes in the US was estimated at 35 million hectares (87.9 million acres),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      following an increasing trend since 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      About 14% of the harvested corn area is irrigated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">
       Pests
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">
       Insects
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/African_armyworm" title="African armyworm">
        African armyworm
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Spodoptera exempta
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Common armyworm (
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudaletia_unipuncta" title="Pseudaletia unipuncta">
         Pseudaletia unipuncta
        </a>
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_earwig" title="Common earwig">
        Common earwig
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Forficula auricularia
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn_delphacid" title="Corn delphacid">
        Corn delphacid
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Peregrinus maidis
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn_leaf_aphid" title="Corn leaf aphid">
        Corn leaf aphid
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Rhopalosiphum maidis
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Corn rootworms (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Diabrotica" title="Diabrotica">
         Diabrotica
        </a>
        spp
       </i>
       ) including
       <a href="/wiki/Western_corn_rootworm" title="Western corn rootworm">
        Western corn rootworm
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Diabrotica virgifera virgifera
       </i>
       LeConte), Northern corn rootworm (
       <i>
        D. barberi
       </i>
       ) and Southern corn rootworm (
       <i>
        D. undecimpunctata howardi
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Euxesta_stigmatias" title="Euxesta stigmatias">
        Corn silkfly
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Euxesta_stigmatias" title="Euxesta stigmatias">
         Euxesta stigmatias
        </a>
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/European_corn_borer" title="European corn borer">
        European corn borer
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Ostrinia nubilalis
       </i>
       ) (ECB)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Fall_armyworm" title="Fall armyworm">
        Fall armyworm
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Spodoptera frugiperda
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Corn earworm/Cotton bollworm (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Helicoverpa_zea" title="Helicoverpa zea">
         Helicoverpa zea
        </a>
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesser_cornstalk_borer" title="Lesser cornstalk borer">
        Lesser cornstalk borer
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Elasmopalpus lignosellus
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Maize_weevil" title="Maize weevil">
        Maize weevil
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Sitophilus zeamais
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Northern armyworm, Oriental armyworm or Rice ear-cutting caterpillar (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mythimna_separata" title="Mythimna separata">
         Mythimna separata
        </a>
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_corn_borer" title="Southwestern corn borer">
        Southwestern corn borer
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Diatraea grandiosella
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Stalk_Borer" title="Stalk Borer">
        Stalk borer
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Papaipema nebris
       </i>
       )
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The susceptibility of maize to the European corn borer and corn rootworms, and the resulting large crop losses which are estimated at a billion dollars worldwide for each pest,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      led to the development of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_plants" title="Transgenic plants">
       transgenics
      </a>
      expressing the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">
        Bacillus thuringiensis
       </a>
      </i>
      toxin. "Bt maize" is widely grown in the United States and has been approved for release in Europe.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">
       Diseases
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_maize_diseases" title="List of maize diseases">
       List of maize diseases
      </a>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Rust_(fungus)" title="Rust (fungus)">
        Rust
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Corn_smut" title="Corn smut">
        Corn smut
       </a>
       or common smut (
       <i>
        Ustilago maydis
       </i>
       ): a fungal disease, known in
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       as
       <i>
        huitlacoche
       </i>
       , which is prized by some as a gourmet delicacy in itself
      </li>
      <li>
       Northern corn leaf blight
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.extension.purdue.edu/extmedia/BP/BP-84-W.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        (Purdue Extension site)
       </a>
       <a class="external text" href="https://www.pioneer.com/home/site/us/agronomy/crop-management/corn-insect-disease/northern-leaf-blight" rel="nofollow">
        (Pioneer site)
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_corn_leaf_blight" title="Southern corn leaf blight">
        Southern corn leaf blight
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Maize_dwarf_mosaic_virus" title="Maize dwarf mosaic virus">
        Maize dwarf mosaic virus
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Maize_streak_virus" title="Maize streak virus">
        Maize streak virus
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Stewart%27s_wilt" title="Stewart's wilt">
        Stewart's wilt
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Pantoea stewartii
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Goss's wilt (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Clavibacter_michiganensis" title="Clavibacter michiganensis">
         Clavibacter michiganensis
        </a>
       </i>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grey_leaf_spot" title="Grey leaf spot">
        Grey leaf spot
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Mal de Río Cuarto virus (MRCV)
      </li>
      <li>
       Stalk rot
      </li>
      <li>
       Ear rot
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">
       Uses
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Human_food">
       Human food
       <span id="Food">
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg">

        </a>
        over an open flame in
        <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">

        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sweet_White_Corn.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="640" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sweet_White_Corn.jpg/220px-Sweet_White_Corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sweet_White_Corn.jpg/330px-Sweet_White_Corn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sweet_White_Corn.jpg/440px-Sweet_White_Corn.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
