        Cut white sweet corn
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3044" data-file-width="3601" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png/220px-Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png/330px-Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png/440px-Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Dried maize
        <a href="/wiki/Mote_(food)" title="Mote (food)">
         <i>
          mote
         </i>
        </a>
        , also known as
        <a href="/wiki/Hominy" title="Hominy">
         hominy
        </a>
        , is used in
        <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">
         Mexican cuisine
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize and
      <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">
       cornmeal
      </a>
      (ground dried maize) constitute a
      <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">
       staple food
      </a>
      in many regions of the world.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is central to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_food" title="Mexican food">
       Mexican food
      </a>
      . Virtually every dish in Mexican cuisine uses maize. In the form of grain or cornmeal, maize is the main ingredient of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tortillas" title="Tortillas">
       tortillas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tamale" title="Tamale">
       tamales
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pozole" title="Pozole">
       pozole
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Atole" title="Atole">
       atole
      </a>
      and all the dishes based on them, like
      <a href="/wiki/Taco" title="Taco">
       tacos
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Quesadilla" title="Quesadilla">
       quesadillas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chilaquiles" title="Chilaquiles">
       chilaquiles
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Enchilada" title="Enchilada">
       enchiladas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tostada_(tortilla)" title="Tostada (tortilla)">
       tostadas
      </a>
      and many more. In Mexico even a fungus of maize, known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huitlacoche" title="Huitlacoche">
       huitlacoche
      </a>
      is considered a delicacy.
     </p>
     <p>
      Introduced into Africa by the Portuguese in the 16th century, maize has become Africa's most important staple food crop.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Maize meal is made into a thick
      <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">
       porridge
      </a>
      in many cultures: from the
      <a href="/wiki/Polenta" title="Polenta">
       polenta
      </a>
      of Italy, the
      <i>
       angu
      </i>
      of Brazil, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/M%C4%83m%C4%83lig%C4%83" title="Mămăligă">
        mămăligă
       </a>
      </i>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">
       Romania
      </a>
      , to
      <a href="/wiki/Mush_(cornmeal)" title="Mush (cornmeal)">
       cornmeal mush
      </a>
      in the US (and
      <a href="/wiki/Hominy" title="Hominy">
       hominy
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Grits" title="Grits">
       grits
      </a>
      in the South) or the food called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mealie_pap" title="Mealie pap">
       mealie pap
      </a>
      in South Africa and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sadza" title="Sadza">
        sadza
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nshima" title="Nshima">
        nshima
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ugali" title="Ugali">
        ugali
       </a>
      </i>
      in other parts of Africa. Maize meal is also used as a replacement for
      <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">
       wheat
      </a>
      flour, to make
      <a href="/wiki/Cornbread" title="Cornbread">
       cornbread
      </a>
      and other baked products.
      <a href="/wiki/Masa" title="Masa">
       Masa
      </a>
      (cornmeal treated with
      <a href="/wiki/Limewater" title="Limewater">
       limewater
      </a>
      ) is the main ingredient for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tortilla" title="Tortilla">
       tortillas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Atole" title="Atole">
       atole
      </a>
      and many other dishes of Central American food.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">
       Popcorn
      </a>
      consists of kernels of certain varieties that explode when heated, forming fluffy pieces that are eaten as a snack. Roasted dried maize ears with semihardened kernels, coated with a seasoning mixture of fried chopped spring onions with salt added to the oil, is a popular
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snack_food" title="Snack food">
       snack food
      </a>
      in Vietnam.
      <i>
       Cancha
      </i>
      , which are roasted maize chulpe kernels, are a very popular snack food in Peru, and also appears in traditional Peruvian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ceviche" title="Ceviche">
        ceviche
       </a>
      </i>
      . An unleavened bread called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Makki_di_roti" title="Makki di roti">
        makki di roti
       </a>
      </i>
      is a popular bread eaten in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">
       Punjab region
      </a>
      of India and Pakistan.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Chicha" title="Chicha">
        Chicha
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       chicha morada
      </i>
      (purple chicha) are drinks typically made from particular types of maize. The first one is fermented and alcoholic, the second is a soft drink commonly drunk in Peru.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_flakes" title="Corn flakes">
       Corn flakes
      </a>
      are a common
      <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_cereal" title="Breakfast cereal">
       breakfast cereal
      </a>
      in North America and the United Kingdom, and found in many other countries all over the world.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize can also be prepared as
      <a href="/wiki/Hominy" title="Hominy">
       hominy
      </a>
      , in which the kernels are soaked with
      <a href="/wiki/Lye" title="Lye">
       lye
      </a>
      in a process called
      <a href="/wiki/Nixtamalization" title="Nixtamalization">
       nixtamalization
      </a>
      ; or
      <a href="/wiki/Grits" title="Grits">
       grits
      </a>
      , which are coarsely ground hominy. These are commonly eaten in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">
       Southeastern United States
      </a>
      , foods handed down from
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">
       Native Americans
      </a>
      , who called the dish
      <a href="/wiki/Sagamite" title="Sagamite">
       sagamite
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Brazilian dessert
      <a href="/wiki/Canjica_(dish)" title="Canjica (dish)">
       <i>
        canjica
       </i>
      </a>
      is made by boiling maize kernels in sweetened milk. Maize can also be harvested and consumed in the unripe state, when the kernels are fully grown but still soft. Unripe maize must usually be cooked to become palatable; this may be done by simply boiling or roasting the whole ears and eating the kernels right off the cob.
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">
       Sweet corn
      </a>
      , a genetic variety that is high in sugars and low in starch, is usually consumed in the unripe state. Such
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_on_the_cob" title="Corn on the cob">
       corn on the cob
      </a>
      is a common dish in the United States, Canada, United Kingdom,
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      , some parts of South America, and the Balkans, but virtually unheard of in some European countries. Corn on the cob was hawked on the streets of early 19th-century New York City by poor, barefoot "
      <a href="/wiki/Hot_Corn" title="Hot Corn">
       Hot Corn
      </a>
      Girls", who were thus the precursors of
      <a href="/wiki/Hot_dog_cart" title="Hot dog cart">
       hot dog carts
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Churro" title="Churro">
       churro
      </a>
      wagons, and fruit stands seen on the streets of big cities today.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The cooked, unripe kernels may also be shaved off the cob and served as a
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">
       vegetable
      </a>
      in side dishes,
      <a href="/wiki/Salad" title="Salad">
       salads
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Garnish_(food)" title="Garnish (food)">
       garnishes
      </a>
      , etc. Alternatively, the raw unripe kernels may also be grated off the cobs and processed into a variety of cooked dishes, such as maize
      <a href="/wiki/Pur%C3%A9e" title="Purée">
       purée
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tamale" title="Tamale">
       tamales
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pamonha" title="Pamonha">
        pamonhas
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Curau" title="Curau">
        curau
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cake" title="Cake">
       cakes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">
       ice creams
      </a>
      , etc.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is a major source of
      <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">
       starch
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cornstarch" title="Cornstarch">
       Cornstarch
      </a>
      (maize flour) is a major ingredient in home cooking and in many industrialized food products. Maize is also a major source of
      <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">
       cooking oil
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_oil" title="Corn oil">
       corn oil
      </a>
      ) and of maize gluten. Maize starch can be
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">
       hydrolyzed
      </a>
      and enzymatically treated to produce syrups, particularly
      <a href="/wiki/High_fructose_corn_syrup" title="High fructose corn syrup">
       high fructose corn syrup
      </a>
      , a sweetener; and also fermented and distilled to produce
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_alcohol" title="Grain alcohol">
       grain alcohol
      </a>
      . Grain alcohol from maize is traditionally the source of
      <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_whiskey" title="Bourbon whiskey">
       Bourbon whiskey
      </a>
      . Maize is sometimes used as the starch source for
      <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">
       beer
      </a>
      . Within the United States, the usage of maize for human consumption constitutes about 1/40th of the amount grown in the country. In the United States and Canada, maize is mostly grown to feed
      <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">
       livestock
      </a>
      , as forage,
      <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">
       silage
      </a>
      (made by fermentation of chopped green cornstalks), or grain. Maize meal is also a significant ingredient of some commercial animal food products, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Dog_food" title="Dog food">
       dog food
      </a>
      .
     </p>

         maize has 90 grams of seeds

      <tr>
       <td class="wrap" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:90%;background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;">

        </a>
        for adults.

        <span class="nowrap">

          <a class="external text" href="http://ndb.nal.usda.gov/ndb/search/list" rel="nofollow">

          </a>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      In a 100-
      <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">
       gram
      </a>
      serving, maize kernels provide 86
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calories" title="Calories">
       calories
      </a>
      and are a good source (10-19% of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">
       Daily Value
      </a>
      ) of the
      <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">
       B vitamins
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiamin" title="Thiamin">
       thiamin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">
       niacin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pantothenic_acid" title="Pantothenic acid">
       pantothenic acid
      </a>
      (B5) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folate" title="Folate">
       folate
      </a>
      (right table for raw, uncooked kernels,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">
       USDA
      </a>
      Nutrient Database). In moderate amounts, they also supply
      <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">
       dietary fiber
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_minerals" title="Essential minerals">
       essential minerals
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">
       phosphorus
      </a>
      whereas other nutrients are in low amounts (see table on right).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals">
       Chemicals
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Starch from maize can also be made into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastics" title="Plastics">
       plastics
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabric" title="Fabric">
       fabrics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Adhesive" title="Adhesive">
       adhesives
      </a>
      , and many other chemical products.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_steep_liquor" title="Corn steep liquor">
       corn steep liquor
      </a>
      , a plentiful watery byproduct of maize
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wet_milling" title="Wet milling">
       wet milling
      </a>
      process, is widely used in the
      <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">
       biochemical industry
      </a>
      and research as a culture medium to grow many kinds of
      <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">
       microorganisms
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-a-84">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Chrysanthemin" title="Chrysanthemin">
       Chrysanthemin
      </a>
      is found in
      <a href="/wiki/Purple_corn" title="Purple corn">
       purple corn
      </a>
      and is used as a food coloring.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bio-fuel">
       Bio-fuel
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_ethanol" title="Corn ethanol">
       Corn ethanol
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_stover" title="Corn stover">
       Corn stover
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      "Feed maize" is being used increasingly for heating;
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      specialized
      <a href="/wiki/Pellet_stove" title="Pellet stove">
       corn stoves
      </a>
      (similar to
      <a href="/wiki/Wood_fuel" title="Wood fuel">
       wood stoves
      </a>
      ) are available and use either feed maize or wood pellets to generate heat. Maize cobs are also used as a
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">
       biomass
      </a>
      fuel source. Maize is relatively cheap and home-heating furnaces have been developed which use maize kernels as a fuel. They feature a large hopper that feeds the uniformly sized maize kernels (or wood pellets or
      <a href="/wiki/Cherry" title="Cherry">
       cherry
      </a>
      pits) into the fire.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is increasingly used as a feedstock for the production of
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">
       ethanol fuel
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Ethanol is mixed with gasoline to decrease the amount of pollutants emitted when used to fuel motor vehicles. High fuel prices in mid-2007 led to higher demand for ethanol, which in turn led to higher prices paid to farmers for maize. This led to the 2007 harvest being one of the most profitable maize crops in modern history for farmers. Because of the relationship between fuel and maize, prices paid for the crop now tend to track the price of oil.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The price of food is affected to a certain degree by the use of maize for biofuel production. The cost of transportation, production, and marketing are a large portion (80%) of the price of food in the United States. Higher energy costs affect these costs, especially transportation. The increase in food prices the consumer has been seeing is mainly due to the higher energy cost. The effect of biofuel production on other food crop prices is indirect. Use of maize for biofuel production increases the demand, and therefore price of maize. This, in turn, results in farm acreage being diverted from other food crops to maize production. This reduces the supply of the other food crops and increases their prices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1909" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG/220px-Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG/330px-Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG/440px-Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Farm-based maize silage digester located near
        <a href="/wiki/Neum%C3%BCnster" title="Neumünster">
         Neumünster
        </a>
        in Germany, 2007. Green inflatable biogas holder is shown on top of the digester
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize is widely used in Germany as a feedstock for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digesters" title="Anaerobic digesters">
       biogas plants
      </a>
      . Here the maize is harvested, shredded then placed in
      <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">
       silage
      </a>
      clamps from which it is fed into the biogas plants. This process makes use of the whole plant rather than simply using the kernels as in the production of fuel ethanol.
     </p>
     <p>
      A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomass_gasification" title="Biomass gasification">
       biomass gasification
      </a>
      power plant in Strem near
      <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCssing" title="Güssing">
       Güssing
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Burgenland" title="Burgenland">
       Burgenland
      </a>
      , Austria, began in 2005. Research is being done to make
      <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">
       diesel
      </a>
      out of the biogas by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer_Tropsch" title="Fischer Tropsch">
       Fischer Tropsch
      </a>
      method.
     </p>
     <p>
      Increasingly, ethanol is being used at low concentrations (10% or less) as an additive in
      <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">
       gasoline
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasohol" title="Gasohol">
       gasohol
      </a>
      ) for motor fuels to increase the
      <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">
       octane rating
      </a>
      , lower pollutants, and reduce petroleum use (what is nowadays also known as "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">
       biofuels
      </a>
      " and has been generating an intense debate regarding the human beings' necessity of new sources of energy, on the one hand, and the need to maintain, in regions such as Latin America, the food habits and culture which has been the essence of civilizations such as the one originated in Mesoamerica; the entry, January 2008, of maize among the commercial agreements of
      <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">
       NAFTA
      </a>
      has increased this debate, considering the bad labor conditions of workers in the fields, and mainly the fact that NAFTA "opened the doors to the import of maize from the United States, where the farmers who grow it receive multimillion dollar subsidies and other government supports. (...) According to OXFAM UK, after NAFTA went into effect, the price of maize in Mexico fell 70% between 1994 and 2001. The number of farm jobs dropped as well: from 8.1 million in 1993 to 6.8 million in 2002. Many of those who found themselves without work were small-scale maize growers.").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, introduction in the northern latitudes of the US of
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.aces.uiuc.edu/news/stories/news4169.html" rel="nofollow">
       tropical maize for biofuels
      </a>
      , and not for human or animal consumption, may potentially alleviate this.
     </p>
     <p>
      As a result of the
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">
       US federal government
      </a>
      announcing its production target of 35 billion US gallons (130,000,000 m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ) of biofuels by 2017, ethanol production will grow to 7 billion US gallons (26,000,000 m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ) by 2010, up from 4.5 billion in 2006, boosting ethanol's share of maize demand in the US from 22.6 percent to 36.1 percent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ornamental_and_other_uses">
       Ornamental and other uses
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_construction" title="Corn construction">
       Corn construction
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some forms of the plant are occasionally grown for ornamental use in the garden. For this purpose, variegated and colored leaf forms as well as those with colorful ears are used.
     </p>
     <p>
      Corncobs can be hollowed out and treated to make inexpensive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_pipe_(tobacco)" title="Smoking pipe (tobacco)">
       smoking pipes
      </a>
      , first manufactured in the United States in 1869.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CornKernelBox.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/CornKernelBox.jpg/220px-CornKernelBox.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/CornKernelBox.jpg/330px-CornKernelBox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/CornKernelBox.jpg/440px-CornKernelBox.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CornKernelBox.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Children playing in a maize kernel box
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An unusual use for maize is to create a "
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_maze" title="Corn maze">
       corn maze
      </a>
      " (or "maize maze") as a tourist attraction. The idea of a maize maze was introduced by the American Maze Company who created a maze in
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">
       Pennsylvania
      </a>
      in 1993.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Traditional mazes are most commonly grown using
      <a href="/wiki/Taxus" title="Taxus">
       yew
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hedge_(gardening)" title="Hedge (gardening)">
       hedges
      </a>
      , but these take several years to mature. The rapid growth of a field of maize allows a maze to be laid out using
      <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">
       GPS
      </a>
      at the start of a growing season and for the maize to grow tall enough to obstruct a visitor's line of sight by the start of the summer. In Canada and the US, these are popular in many farming communities.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize kernels can be used in place of sand in a
      <a href="/wiki/Sandpit" title="Sandpit">
       sandboxlike
      </a>
      enclosure for children's play.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corn_box_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-corn_box-90">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Stigmas from female maize flowers, popularly called
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_silk" title="Corn silk">
       corn silk
      </a>
      , are sold as
      <a href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">
       herbal supplements
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize is used as a
      <a href="/wiki/Bait_(luring_substance)" title="Bait (luring substance)">
       fish bait
      </a>
      , called "dough balls". It is particularly popular in Europe for
      <a href="/wiki/Coarse_fishing" title="Coarse fishing">
       coarse fishing
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Additionally, feed corn is sometimes used by hunters to bait animals such as deer or wild hogs.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fodder">
       Fodder
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Maize produces a greater quantity of biomass than other
      <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">
       cereal
      </a>
      plants, which is used for
      <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">
       fodder
      </a>
      . Digestibility and palatability are higher when ensiled and fermented, rather than dried.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commodity">
       Commodity
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Maize is bought and sold by investors and price speculators as a tradable commodity using corn
      <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">
       futures contracts
      </a>
      . These "futures" are traded on the
      <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">
       Chicago Board of Trade
      </a>
      (CBOT) under
      <a href="/wiki/Ticker_symbol" title="Ticker symbol">
       ticker symbol
      </a>
      <b>
       C
      </b>
      . They are delivered every year in March, May, July, September, and December.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_usage_breakdown">
       United States usage breakdown
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The breakdown of usage of the 12.1-billion-
      <a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">
       bushel
      </a>
      (307-million-tonne) 2008 US maize crop was as follows, according to the World Agricultural Supply and Demand Estimates Report by the USDA.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">

     </table>
     <p>
      In the US since 2009/2010, maize feedstock use for ethanol production has somewhat exceeded direct use for livestock feed; maize use for fuel ethanol was 5,130 million bushels (130 million tonnes) in the 2013/2014 marketing year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A fraction of the maize feedstock dry matter used for ethanol production is usefully recovered as DDGS (dried distillers grains with solubles). In the 2010/2011 marketing year, about 29.1 million tonnes of DDGS were fed to US livestock and poultry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-94">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because starch utilization in fermentation for ethanol production leaves other grain constituents more concentrated in the residue, the feed value per kg of DDGS, with regard to ruminant-metabolizable energy and protein, exceeds that of the grain. Feed value for monogastric animals, such as swine and poultry, is somewhat lower than for ruminants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_94-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-94">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <p>
      Ch. = Choline; Ca = Calcium; Fe = Iron; Mg = Magnesium; P = Phosphorus; K = Potassium; Na = Sodium; Zn = Zinc; Cu = Copper; Mn = Manganese; Se = Selenium; %DV = % daily value i.e. % of DRI (
      <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">
       Dietary Reference Intake
      </a>
      ) Note: All nutrient values including protein and fiber are in %DV per 100 grams of the food item. Significant values are highlighted in light Gray color and bold letters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [94]

      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cooking reduction = % Maximum typical reduction in nutrients due to boiling without draining for ovo-lacto-vegetables group
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NutritionalEffectsOfFoodProcessing_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NutritionalEffectsOfFoodProcessing-98">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Q = Quality of Protein in terms of completeness without adjusting for digestability.

     <p>
      The following table shows the nutrient content of maize and major staple foods in a raw harvested form. Raw forms are not edible and cannot be digested. These must be sprouted, or prepared and cooked for human consumption. In sprouted or cooked form, the relative nutritional and anti-nutritional contents of each of these staples are different from that of raw form of these staples reported in the table below.

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">
       Hazards
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pellagra">
       Pellagra
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Pellagra" title="Pellagra">
       Pellagra
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      When maize was first introduced into farming systems other than those used by traditional native-American peoples, it was generally welcomed with enthusiasm for its productivity. However, a widespread problem of malnutrition soon arose wherever maize was introduced as a
      <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">
       staple food
      </a>
      . This was a mystery, since these types of malnutrition were not normally seen among the indigenous Americans, for whom maize was the principal staple food.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pellagra_mystery_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pellagra_mystery-100">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It was eventually discovered that the indigenous Americans had learned to soak maize in
      <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">
       alkali
      </a>
      -water—made with ashes and lime (
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">
       calcium oxide
      </a>
      ) since at least 1200–1500 BC by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamericans" title="Mesoamericans">
       Mesoamericans
      </a>
      and North Americans—which liberates the B-vitamin
      <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">
       niacin
      </a>
      , the lack of which was the underlying cause of the condition known as
      <a href="/wiki/Pellagra" title="Pellagra">
       pellagra
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Maize was introduced into the diet of nonindigenous Americans without the necessary cultural knowledge acquired over thousands of years in the Americas. In the late 19th century, pellagra reached epidemic proportions in parts of the southern US, as medical researchers debated two theories for its origin: the deficiency theory (which was eventually shown to be true) said that pellagra was due to a deficiency of some nutrient, and the germ theory said that pellagra was caused by a germ transmitted by stable flies. A third theory, promoted by the eugenicist
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Davenport" title="Charles Davenport">
       Charles Davenport
      </a>
      , held that people only contracted pellagra if they were susceptible to it due to certain "constitutional, inheritable" traits of the affected individual.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Once alkali processing and dietary variety were understood and applied, pellagra disappeared in the developed world. The development of high lysine maize and the promotion of a more balanced diet have also contributed to its demise. Pellagra still exists today in food-poor areas and refugee camps where people survive on donated maize.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Allergy">
       Allergy
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Maize contains
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipid_transfer_proteins" title="Lipid transfer proteins">
       lipid transfer protein
      </a>
      , an indigestible protein that survives cooking. This protein has been linked to a rare and understudied
      <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">
       allergy
      </a>
      to maize in humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The allergic reaction can cause skin rash, swelling or itching of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mucous_membranes" title="Mucous membranes">
       mucous membranes
      </a>
      , diarrhea, vomiting,
      <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">
       asthma
      </a>
      and, in severe cases,
      <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">
       anaphylaxis
      </a>
      . It is unclear how common this allergy is in the general population.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Art">
       Art
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:333px;max-width:333px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mochica_Corn.jpg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="258" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Mochica_Corn.jpg/175px-Mochica_Corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Mochica_Corn.jpg 1.5x" width="175"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Gold maize. Moche culture 300 A.D.,
         <a href="/wiki/Larco_Museum" title="Larco Museum">
          Larco Museum
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">
          Lima
         </a>
         , Peru
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CornWaterTower.JPG">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/CornWaterTower.JPG/150px-CornWaterTower.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/CornWaterTower.JPG/225px-CornWaterTower.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/CornWaterTower.JPG/300px-CornWaterTower.JPG 2x" width="150"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         <a href="/wiki/Water_tower" title="Water tower">
          Water tower
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_Minnesota" title="Rochester, Minnesota">
          Rochester, Minnesota
         </a>
         being painted as an ear of maize
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Maize has been an essential crop in the
      <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">
       Andes
      </a>
      since the
      <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">
       pre-Columbian era
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">
       Moche
      </a>
      culture from Northern
      <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
       Peru
      </a>
      made ceramics from earth, water, and fire. This pottery was a sacred substance, formed in significant shapes and used to represent important themes. Maize represented anthropomorphically as well as naturally.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the United States, maize ears along with tobacco leaves are carved into the capitals of columns in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">
       United States Capitol
      </a>
      building. Maize itself is sometimes used for temporary architectural detailing when the intent is to celebrate the fall season, local agricultural productivity and culture. Bundles of dried maize stalks are often displayed often along with pumpkins, gourds and straw in autumnal displays outside homes and businesses. A well-known example of architectural use is the
      <a href="/wiki/Corn_Palace" title="Corn Palace">
       Corn Palace
      </a>
      in Mitchell, South Dakota, which uses cobs and ears of colored maize to implement a mural design that is recycled annually.
     </p>
     <p>
      A maize stalk with two ripe ears is depicted on the
      <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">
       reverse
      </a>
      of the Croatian 1
      <a href="/wiki/Croatian_kuna" title="Croatian kuna">
       lipa
      </a>
      coin, minted since 1993.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

       <li id="cite_note-Ensminger-16">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Ensminger_16-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Ensminger_16-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation book">
          Ensminger, Audrey H. (1994).
          <i>
           Foods and Nutrition Encyclopedia, 2nd ed
          </i>
          . CRC Press. p.
          <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=XMA9gYIj-C4C&amp;pg=PA479#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">
           479
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8493-8980-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-8493-8980-1">
           0-8493-8980-1
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           The word "maize" is preferred in international usage because in many countries the term "corn", the name by which the plant is known in the United States, is synonymous with the leading cereal grain; thus, in England "corn" refers to wheat, and in Scotland and Ireland it refers to oats.
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMaize&amp;rft.aufirst=Audrey+H.&amp;rft.aulast=Ensminger&amp;rft.btitle=Foods+and+Nutrition+Encyclopedia%2C+2nd+ed.&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-8493-8980-1&amp;rft.pages=479&amp;rft.pub=CRC+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
